<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Streaming with Tool Use: Architecture & Implementation

Great question! Streaming with tool calls has two distinct patterns depending on your needs. Let me break down what you need to know.

## The Core Concept

When Claude makes tool calls with streaming enabled, you get:
1. **Real-time token streaming** for Claude's text responses
2. **Fine-grained tool streaming** (GA) — tool call parameters stream incrementally as Claude thinks
3. **Server-side tool execution** — you intercept tool calls and execute them
4. **Streaming tool results back** — Claude processes results and streams the final response

This is different from traditional request-response: you're managing a conversation loop where each message may trigger tools.

---

## Pattern 1: Streaming UI with Server-Side Tool Execution (Recommended)

This is the most common pattern — stream Claude's thinking to the user while handling tools behind the scenes.

### Architecture Flow

```
User Input
    ↓
Send to Claude (stream: true)
    ↓
[Stream text deltas to UI] ← content_block_delta events
    ↓
Claude decides to call tool → tool_use block appears in stream
    ↓
[You execute tool server-side]
    ↓
Send tool result back to Claude → get new stream
    ↓
[Stream final response to UI]
```

### Implementation (TypeScript with Anthropic SDK)

```typescript
import Anthropic from "@anthropic-ai/sdk";

const client = new Anthropic();

async function streamWithTools(userMessage: string) {
  const messages: Anthropic.MessageParam[] = [
    { role: "user", content: userMessage },
  ];

  while (true) {
    const stream = await client.messages.stream({
      model: "claude-opus-4-6",
      max_tokens: 4096,
      tools: [
        {
          name: "web_search",
          description: "Search the web for information",
          input_schema: {
            type: "object",
            properties: {
              query: {
                type: "string",
                description: "The search query",
              },
            },
            required: ["query"],
          },
        },
        {
          name: "code_execution",
          description: "Execute Python code",
          input_schema: {
            type: "object",
            properties: {
              code: {
                type: "string",
                description: "Python code to execute",
              },
            },
            required: ["code"],
          },
        },
      ],
      messages,
    });

    let toolUseBlocks: Anthropic.ToolUseBlock[] = [];
    let textContent = "";
    let hasToolUse = false;

    // Process the stream
    for await (const event of stream) {
      if (event.type === "content_block_start") {
        if (event.content_block.type === "tool_use") {
          hasToolUse = true;
          console.log(`\n[Tool Call] ${event.content_block.name}`);
        }
      }

      if (event.type === "content_block_delta") {
        if (event.delta.type === "text_delta") {
          // Stream text to UI in real-time
          process.stdout.write(event.delta.text);
          textContent += event.delta.text;
        }

        if (event.delta.type === "input_json_delta") {
          // Fine-grained streaming of tool parameters
          // (informational — parameters accumulate in the block)
          console.log(`[Param update] ${event.delta.input_json}`);
        }
      }

      if (event.type === "content_block_stop") {
        if (event.index >= 0 && stream.currentEvent()?.message?.content) {
          const block = stream.currentEvent().message.content[event.index];
          if (block?.type === "tool_use") {
            toolUseBlocks.push(block as Anthropic.ToolUseBlock);
          }
        }
      }
    }

    // Get the final message from the stream
    const response = stream.finalMessage();

    // If Claude made tool calls, execute them and loop
    if (
      response.stop_reason === "tool_use" &&
      response.content.some((block) => block.type === "tool_use")
    ) {
      // Add Claude's response (with tool calls) to conversation
      messages.push({ role: "assistant", content: response.content });

      // Execute tools and collect results
      const toolResults: Anthropic.ToolResultBlockParam[] = [];

      for (const block of response.content) {
        if (block.type === "tool_use") {
          console.log(`\n[Executing] ${block.name}`);
          let result: string;

          try {
            if (block.name === "web_search") {
              // Simulate web search
              result = `Search results for "${
                (block.input as { query: string }).query
              }": Found 10 results...`;
            } else if (block.name === "code_execution") {
              // Execute code (in production, use sandbox)
              const code = (block.input as { code: string }).code;
              result = `Executed code. Output: Success`;
            } else {
              result = "Tool not implemented";
            }
          } catch (error) {
            result = `Error: ${error instanceof Error ? error.message : "Unknown error"}`;
          }

          toolResults.push({
            type: "tool_result",
            tool_use_id: block.id,
            content: result,
          });
        }
      }

      // Add tool results to conversation
      messages.push({ role: "user", content: toolResults });

      // Loop back to stream Claude's response to the tool results
      console.log("\n[Waiting for Claude to process tool results...]\n");
      continue; // Go back to the top of the while loop
    }

    // Claude finished (stop_reason === "end_turn"), exit loop
    break;
  }

  console.log("\n[Done]");
}

// Run it
streamWithTools("Search for the latest Claude 4.6 features and summarize them");
```

### Key Points

- **`stream: true`** enables real-time deltas
- **Fine-grained tool streaming** (GA) means `input_json_delta` events arrive as Claude computes tool parameters — useful for UI that shows "Claude is preparing to call X tool"
- **Loop pattern**: stream → check `stop_reason` → if `"tool_use"`, execute tools → add results to messages → stream again
- **`finalMessage()`** gives you the accumulated response after streaming ends

---

## Pattern 2: Streaming Tool Parameters for UI Feedback (Advanced)

If you want to show the user what tool Claude *intends* to call before execution:

```typescript
// In the stream loop, react to input_json_delta
for await (const event of stream) {
  if (
    event.type === "content_block_delta" &&
    event.delta.type === "input_json_delta"
  ) {
    // Partial tool input received
    const toolUse = stream.currentEvent().message?.content?.[event.index];
    if (toolUse?.type === "tool_use") {
      // UI could show: "Claude will search for: [partial query]..."
      updateUI({
        type: "tool_preview",
        name: toolUse.name,
        input: toolUse.input, // Accumulates as deltas arrive
      });
    }
  }
}
```

This is useful for:
- Showing user what Claude is about to do
- Pre-caching API keys or permissions
- Building trust ("Claude will now search for X")

---

```typescript
// In your tool execution block:
for (const block of response.content) {
  if (block.type === "tool_use") {
    const toolResult = await executeTool(block.name, block.input);

    // Directly add to messages WITHOUT streaming back to Claude
    // Use this when you're batching multiple tool calls
    messages.push({
      role: "user",
      content: [
        {
          type: "tool_result",
          tool_use

</details>

# Fast Mode for Claude Opus 4.6

Great question! Fast mode is a beta feature that significantly speeds up Opus 4.6's output generation. Here's what you need to know:

## How It Works

Fast mode uses optimized inference to deliver responses **2.5x faster** than standard Opus 4.6. You enable it by:

1. Setting `speed: "fast"` in your API request
2. Including the beta header: `betas: ["fast-mode-2026-02-01"]`

```python
import anthropic

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

message = client.messages.create(
    model="claude-opus-4-20250805",
    max_tokens=1024,
    speed="fast",  # Enable fast mode
    messages=[
        {"role": "user", "content": "Explain quantum computing briefly"}
    ],
    betas=["fast-mode-2026-02-01"],  # Required beta header
)

print(message.content[0].text)
```

## Pricing

**Fast mode costs 6x the standard Opus 4.6 rate:**

| Metric | Standard Opus 4.6 | Fast Mode Opus 4.6 |
|--------|-------------------|-------------------|
| Input | $5 / MTok | $30 / MTok |
| Output | $25 / MTok | $150 / MTok |

Despite the higher per-token cost, the **2.5x speed improvement** often reduces total latency more effectively than queuing requests, making it worthwhile for time-sensitive applications.

## Key Limitations & Considerations

### What's Affected
- **Speed gain**: Output generation is 2.5x faster
- **Quality**: Still Opus 4.6-level reasoning (adaptive thinking still available)
- **Context**: Works with 200K context; 1M context support unclear (confirm with your use case)

### What's NOT Affected
- **Input processing** doesn't speed up — only output generation
- **Token costs scale linearly** — you still pay for all tokens, just at a higher rate
- **Thinking modes** — You can still use `thinking: {"type": "adaptive"}` with fast mode

### When to Use Fast Mode

✅ **Good use cases:**
- Real-time chat applications where latency matters more than cost
- Time-sensitive analysis (stock trading alerts, incident response)
- User-facing applications where 2.5s vs 1s response time impacts UX
- Streaming responses (combine with `stream: true` for responsive partial content)

❌ **Poor use cases:**
- Batch processing (use standard Opus + Batch API for 50% discount instead)
- Cost-sensitive workflows (Sonnet 4.6 + fast mode may be cheaper)
- Non-time-critical tasks

## Optimization Tips

1. **Combine with streaming**: Use `stream: true` for immediate partial output while fast mode generates the rest
   ```python
   message = client.messages.create(
       model="claude-opus-4-20250805",
       max_tokens=1024,
       speed="fast",
       stream=True,  # Stream output as it's generated
       messages=[...],
       betas=["fast-mode-2026-02-01"],
   )
   ```

2. **Reduce max_tokens strategically**: Only request what you need to cut token costs
3. **Compare Sonnet 4.6 vs Opus fast**: Sonnet is cheaper and faster; run A/B tests for your workload
4. **Use effort levels**: Pair with `effort: "low"` for simple tasks to potentially reduce latency further

## Current Status

Fast mode is **beta** (as of March 2026). Features may change, and Anthropic may adjust pricing or performance. Monitor your usage closely in production.

Would you like help benchmarking fast mode against standard Opus or Sonnet 4.6 for your specific use case?

</details>

**Prompt:** I have a multi-turn conversation app and I want to use prompt caching without manually managing cache breakpoints as the conversation grows. Is there a way to make caching automatic?

#### Control Response

- Completeness keywords matched: ['block-level', '10%']
- Deprecated patterns found: []
- Judge — Accuracy: 0, Completeness: 1, Actionability: 1

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Automatic Prompt Caching for Multi-Turn Conversations

Unfortunately, **Claude's prompt caching doesn't automatically manage cache breakpoints as conversations grow**. You need to explicitly define where cache breaks occur. However, you can **automate this management** in your application layer.

## Recommended Approach: Automatic Cache Segmentation

Here's a pattern that simulates automatic caching:

```python
import anthropic

class CachedConversationManager:
    def __init__(self, cache_size_threshold=10000):
        self.client = anthropic.Anthropic()
        self.conversation_history = []
        self.cache_size_threshold = cache_size_threshold
        self.system_prompt = "You are a helpful assistant."
    
    def _should_create_checkpoint(self, current_history_length):
        """Determine if we should create a cache checkpoint"""
        return current_history_length > self.cache_size_threshold
    
    def _build_cached_messages(self):
        """Build messages with automatic cache control breakpoints"""
        messages = []
        accumulated_length = 0
        
        for i, msg in enumerate(self.conversation_history):
            messages.append(msg)
            accumulated_length += len(msg["content"])
            
            # Add cache breakpoint at threshold, but not on the last message
            if (accumulated_length > self.cache_size_threshold and 
                i < len(self.conversation_history) - 1):
                messages[-1]["cache_control"] = {"type": "ephemeral"}
                accumulated_length = 0
        
        return messages
    
    def add_message(self, role, content):
        """Add message to conversation history"""
        self.conversation_history.append({
            "role": role,
            "content": content
        })
    
    def get_response(self, user_message):
        """Get response with automatic cache management"""
        self.add_message("user", user_message)
        
        # Build messages with cache breakpoints
        messages = self._build_cached_messages()
        
        response = self.client.messages.create(
            model="claude-3-5-sonnet-20241022",
            max_tokens=1024,
            system=[
                {
                    "type": "text",
                    "text": self.system_prompt,
                    "cache_control": {"type": "ephemeral"}
                }
            ],
            messages=messages
        )
        
        assistant_message = response.content[0].text
        self.add_message("assistant", assistant_message)
        
        return assistant_message

# Usage
manager = CachedConversationManager(cache_size_threshold=5000)

manager.add_message("user", "Tell me about Python")
response = manager.get_response("What are the key features?")
print(response)
```

## Alternative: Token-Based Caching

For more precise control, track tokens instead of character count:

```python
def _estimate_tokens(self, text):
    """Rough estimate: ~4 characters per token"""
    return len(text) // 4

def _build_cached_messages(self):
    messages = []
    token_count = 0
    
    for i, msg in enumerate(self.conversation_history):
        messages.append(msg.copy())
        msg_tokens = self._estimate_tokens(msg["content"])
        token_count += msg_tokens
        
        # Add breakpoint every ~5000 tokens
        if token_count > 5000 and i < len(self.conversation_history) - 1:
            messages[-1]["cache_control"] = {"type": "ephemeral"}
            token_count = 0
    
    return messages
```

## Key Limitations to Understand

| Aspect | Limitation |
|--------|-----------|
| **Automatic Detection** | You must define thresholds; Claude doesn't do this for you |
| **Cache Window** | Only recent ~3K tokens can be cached (ephemeral) |
| **Breakpoint Placement** | Caching is best between logical conversation sections |
| **Cost Savings** | You only save on cached tokens on *subsequent* requests |

## Best Practices

1. **Segment by conversation topic** rather than arbitrary token counts
2. **Don't cache the last few messages** (they change frequently)
3. **Test cache hits** by examining `usage.cache_read_input_tokens` in responses
4. **Set reasonable thresholds** (5K-10K tokens is typical)

This approach gives you "automatic" management at the application level while respecting Claude's caching constraints.

</details>

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Automatic Prompt Caching in Multi-Turn Conversations

Yes! Claude supports **automatic caching** via the `cache_control` parameter at the request level, which is perfect for multi-turn conversations where you don't want to manually track cache breakpoints.

## How Automatic Caching Works

When you add `cache_control: {"type": "ephemeral"}` at the request level, Claude automatically:
- Identifies natural cacheable boundaries (typically system prompt + conversation history up to recent messages)
- Creates cache breakpoints without you specifying them manually
- Reuses cached content across requests (5-minute TTL for ephemeral cache)

This is ideal because your conversation grows organically—Claude handles the cache management.

## Implementation Example

```python
import anthropic

client = anthropic.Anthropic()
conversation_history = []

def chat(user_message):
    """Send a message and automatically cache conversation context."""
    conversation_history.append({
        "role": "user",
        "content": user_message
    })
    
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-opus-4-6-20250514",
        max_tokens=1024,
        system="You are a helpful assistant. Answer questions concisely.",
        messages=conversation_history,
        cache_control={"type": "ephemeral"}  # ← Automatic caching
    )
    
    assistant_message = response.content[0].text
    conversation_history.append({
        "role": "assistant",
        "content": assistant_message
    })
    
    # Check cache usage
    usage = response.usage
    print(f"Input tokens: {usage.input_tokens}")
    print(f"Cache creation tokens: {usage.cache_creation_input_tokens}")
    print(f"Cache read tokens: {usage.cache_read_input_tokens}")
    
    return assistant_message

# Multi-turn conversation
chat("What is photosynthesis?")
chat("How does it relate to climate change?")
chat("What can we do about it?")  # Earlier context reused from cache
```

## How Cache Payoff Works

- **Turn 1**: System prompt + messages cached (pays 25% cache creation cost)
- **Turn 2 onwards**: System prompt + conversation history reused from cache (10% cache read cost)

For a conversation with 10K tokens of system + history:
- **Without caching**: 10K tokens per request
- **With caching**: 2.5K tokens (creation, turn 1) + 1K tokens (reads, turns 2+)
- **Savings**: ~80% after first request

## Key Considerations

| Aspect | Details |
|--------|---------|
| **TTL** | 5 minutes (ephemeral). Cache persists across requests in same session. |
| **Breakpoint placement** | Claude auto-manages—typically system + older messages cached, recent messages not cached for freshness. |
| **Growth handling** | As conversation grows, older messages shift into cache automatically. Recent context stays mutable. |
| **Staleness** | Not an issue—cache is only reused within 5 minutes of creation. |

## Advanced: Combine with Explicit Caching

If you need fine-grained control (e.g., always cache a large knowledge base but refresh conversation context), use block-level `cache_control` alongside request-level:

```python
response = client.messages.create(
    model="claude-opus-4-6-20250514",
    max_tokens=1024,
    system=[
        {
            "type": "text",
            "text": "You are a helpful assistant.",
            "cache_control": {"type": "ephemeral"}  # ← Always cache system
        }
    ],
    messages=conversation_history,
    cache_control={"type": "ephemeral"}  # ← Also auto-cache messages
)
```

## When to Use Automatic vs. Manual Caching

| Scenario | Recommendation |
|----------|---|
| Multi-turn chat, natural growth | **Automatic** (`cache_control` at request level) |
| Static knowledge base + chat | **Both** (block-level for KB, request-level for messages) |
| Complex, custom breakpoint logic | **Manual** (block-level at specific boundaries) |

## Pricing Reminder

- Cache creation: **25% of input token cost**
- Cache read: **10% of input token cost**
- Cache creation happens once; reads recur at 10% for 5 minutes (huge savings on multi-turn)

For a 20-turn conversation with 5K tokens of shared context: automatic caching saves ~4K tokens per turn after the first request—around **$0.06** on Opus 4.6.

**Bottom line**: Use `cache_control: {"type": "ephemeral"}` at request level for all multi-turn apps. It's zero-configuration and handles cache lifecycle automatically.

</details>

# Filtering Web Search Results

Yes—there are two approaches depending on your setup:

## 1. **Dynamic Filtering with Code Execution** (Recommended for most cases)

If you're using the latest **web search** tool (`web_search_20260209` or newer), it automatically integrates with **code execution** to filter results before they enter your context window.

Here's how it works:
- Claude calls web search, gets raw results
- Code execution automatically processes the results
- Irrelevant items are filtered out
- Only curated results reach your context

**Setup:** Just include both tools:

```json
{
  "tools": [
    {"type": "web_search_20260209", "name": "web_search"},
    {"type": "code_execution_20260120", "name": "code_execution"}
  ]
}
```

The filtering happens automatically—Claude will use code execution to post-process results based on relevance to your query.

## 2. **Manual Filtering via Programmatic Tool Calling** (Full control)

If you need explicit control, use **programmatic tool calling** (GA—no beta header needed). You call the web search tool from code execution directly:

```python
# Inside code execution
results = web_search("your query")
# Filter in Python before returning
filtered = [r for r in results if relevance_score(r) > threshold]
```

This lets you:
- Filter by domain, keyword matching, or custom scoring
- Deduplicate results
- Rank by relevance
- Limit result count before they enter the context window

## 3. **Query Optimization** (Preventative)

Before relying on filtering, tighten your search queries:

- Use **specific keywords** instead of broad terms
- Add **exclusions**: `"your topic" -irrelevant_site`
- Use **site operators**: `site:domain.com your query`
- Specify **result type** in your prompt: *"Search for academic papers on X"* vs *"Search news about X"*

---

## Platform-Specific Notes

- **Claude.ai + Claude Code**: Dynamic filtering (`web_search_20260209`) works out of the box when both tools are available
- **API (direct/Bedrock/Vertex)**: Use the dynamic filtering version or implement programmatic tool calling in your client
- **Claude Desktop**: Use the latest web search tool for automatic filtering

The dynamic filtering approach (`web_search_20260209`) is the easiest—it filters irrelevant results *before* they bloat your context window, saving both tokens and reducing noise.

</details>

### Test 5.2: Extension Awareness

**Prompt:** Every time I edit a Python file, I want to make sure it passes our linting rules and type checks. Right now I keep forgetting to run the checks. Is there a way to automate this?

#### Control Response
- Accuracy keywords matched: ['hook', 'automatic']
- Completeness keywords matched: ['command', 'shell', 'edit']
- Deprecated patterns found: []
- Judge — Accuracy: 0, Completeness: 0, Actionability: 1

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Automate Python Linting and Type Checking

Yes! There are several great approaches. Here are the most popular:

## 1. **Git Pre-commit Hooks** (Best for teams)

Use the `pre-commit` framework to run checks before commits:

```bash
pip install pre-commit
```

Create `.pre-commit-config.yaml` in your repo:

```yaml
repos:
  - repo: https://github.com/astral-sh/ruff
    rev: v0.1.0
    hooks:
      - id: ruff
      - id: ruff-format

  - repo: https://github.com/pre-commit/mirrors-mypy
    rev: v1.7.0
    hooks:
      - id: mypy
        additional_dependencies: [types-all]
```

Then run:
```bash
pre-commit install
```

Now checks run automatically before each commit.

## 2. **IDE/Editor Integration** (Best for immediate feedback)

**VS Code:**
- Install "Pylance" or "Python" extension
- Add to `.vscode/settings.json`:
```json
{
  "python.linting.enabled": true,
  "python.linting.ruffEnabled": true,
  "[python]": {
    "editor.formatOnSave": true,
    "editor.codeActionsOnSave": {
      "source.organizeImports": "explicit"
    }
  }
}
```

**PyCharm:**
- Settings → Tools → Python Integrated Tools → enable linters
- File → Settings → Tools → Python Integrated Tools

## 3. **GitHub Actions** (For CI/CD)

Create `.github/workflows/lint.yml`:

```yaml
name: Lint and Type Check
on: [push, pull_request]

jobs:
  lint:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v3
      - uses: actions/setup-python@v4
      - run: pip install ruff mypy
      - run: ruff check .
      - run: ruff format --check .
      - run: mypy .
```

## 4. **File Watcher** (Development convenience)

Use `watchdog` for auto-checking on save:

```bash
pip install watchdog[watchmedo]
```

```bash
watchmedo shell-command \
  --patterns="*.py" \
  --recursive \
  --command='ruff check ${watch_src_path} && mypy ${watch_src_path}' \
  .
```

## Recommendation

**Start with:** Pre-commit hooks + IDE integration
- Catches issues before commits
- Gives real-time feedback while coding
- Enforces standards across your team

**Add:** GitHub Actions for CI/CD safety net

Which tools are you currently using for linting?

</details>

#### Treatment Response
- Accuracy keywords matched: ['hook', 'event', 'automatic', 'deterministic']
- Completeness keywords matched: ['command', 'shell', 'edit']
- Deprecated patterns found: []
- Judge — Accuracy: 1, Completeness: 2, Actionability: 2

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

Yes! This is a perfect use case for **hooks** in Claude Code. Hooks let you run deterministic automation (like linting) after specific events—in your case, after file edits—without needing Claude tokens.

## Solution: Use a File Save Hook

In Claude Code, you can set up a hook that runs automatically whenever you save a Python file. Here's how:

### 1. Create a hook configuration

Add this to your `.claude/hooks.json` (create it if it doesn't exist):

```json
{
  "hooks": [
    {
      "name": "python-lint-on-save",
      "type": "shell",
      "trigger": "file_change",
      "pattern": "**/*.py",
      "command": "bash -c 'echo \"Linting $FILE...\" && python -m pylint \"$FILE\" && python -m mypy \"$FILE\"'"
    }
  ]
}
```

This hook:
- Triggers on any Python file change (`**/*.py`)
- Runs `pylint` + `mypy` automatically
- Outputs results to your terminal
- **Costs 0 tokens** (it's shell automation, not Claude)

### 2. Customize the linting command

Replace the command with whatever your project uses:

**Just ruff (faster):**
```json
"command": "ruff check \"$FILE\" && ruff format --check \"$FILE\""
```

**Just mypy:**
```json
"command": "python -m mypy \"$FILE\""
```

**Pre-commit framework** (if you use it):
```json
"command": "pre-commit run --files \"$FILE\""
```

**Your custom script:**
```json
"command": "bash ./scripts/lint.sh \"$FILE\""
```

### 3. Optional: Add HTTP hook for notifications

If you want Claude to *report* failures in chat (not just shell output), use an HTTP hook:

```json
{
  "name": "report-lint-failures",
  "type": "http",
  "trigger": "file_change",
  "pattern": "**/*.py",
  "url": "http://localhost:3000/lint-check",
  "method": "POST",
  "payload": {
    "file": "$FILE"
  }
}
```

Then Claude can process the response and alert you if linting fails.

---

## Alternative: Use `/loop` for periodic checks

If you want to lint *all* Python files every few minutes (rather than on save):

```
/loop every 2 minutes run pylint src/ && mypy src/
```

This keeps running in the background. Good for catching issues across your codebase.

---

## Why hooks over other options?

| Approach | Cost | When to use |
|----------|------|-----------|
| **Hook** (shell) | **0 tokens** ✓ | Auto-run after every save |
| **/loop** | Low tokens | Periodic checks across codebase |
| **Skill** | Costs tokens | Manual `/lint` command |
| **CI/CD pipeline** | N/A | Team-wide enforcement |

Hooks are best because they're **instant, free, and deterministic**—no Claude involved, just pure automation.

---

## Next steps

1. Create `.claude/hooks.json` with the shell hook above
2. Test by saving a Python file—you should see lint output in your terminal
3. Adjust the `command` to match your linting setup
4. (Optional) Add `/loop` or HTTP hooks if you need more sophisticated workflows

Let me know if you hit any issues with the hook syntax or need help integrating with a specific linter!

</details>

---

# Best Approaches for API Conventions

## 1. **System Prompt (Recommended for consistency)**
Put conventions in your system prompt if you're using the API directly:

```
You are a helpful API documentation assistant for Acme Corp.

Our API conventions:
- JSON fields use camelCase
- All paginated endpoints include: pageNumber, pageSize, totalItems
- Dates use ISO 8601 format (YYYY-MM-DDTHH:mm:ssZ)
- Error responses follow: {code, message, details}
```

**Pros:** Applied to every message, consistent behavior
**Cons:** Uses token budget; needs updates via API calls

## 2. **Context in User Message (Flexible)**
Include it at the start of relevant conversations:

```
[Our API uses camelCase for JSON, ISO 8601 dates, and includes pagination with pageNumber/pageSize/totalItems]

Can you review this endpoint design?
```

**Pros:** Easy to adjust per conversation, clear scope
**Cons:** Manual each time, token overhead if repeated

## 3. **Hybrid Approach (Often best)**
- Put **core, unchanging conventions** in the system prompt
- Include **context-specific details** in user messages
- Works well when conventions rarely change but specific requests vary

## Practical tip:
If using Claude through an application you control, embed conventions in the system prompt. If copy-pasting into Claude.ai conversations, include them in your message for clarity.

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Company API Conventions Storage

Based on your use case, the answer depends on **where** you're using Claude:

## Best Option by Platform

| Platform | Best Choice | Why |
|----------|-------------|-----|
| **Claude Code** | **CLAUDE.md** | Always loaded, ~200 lines, perfect for reference docs |
| **Claude.ai / Desktop** | **Projects** | Persistent context per project, easy to update |
| **API-only** | **System prompt** | Full control, include in every request |

---

## Detailed Recommendations

### Claude Code → **CLAUDE.md**
This is ideal for your use case. Create a `.claude/CLAUDE.md` file (or `CLAUDE.md` in root):

```markdown
# Company API Conventions

## JSON Field Naming
- Use camelCase for all JSON fields
- Example: `createdAt`, `userId`, `isActive`

## Pagination
- Always include pagination in list endpoints
- Required fields: `limit`, `offset`, `total`, `hasMore`
- Default limit: 20, max: 100

## Dates
- Use ISO 8601 format (YYYY-MM-DDTHH:mm:ssZ)
- Always use UTC timezone
- Example: `2024-03-13T14:30:00Z`

## Error Responses
- Use HTTP status codes consistently
- Include `code`, `message`, and `details` fields
```

**Benefits:**
- Always available in the context
- No token cost (loaded automatically)
- Easy to update with `.claude/rules/` if it grows beyond ~200 lines
- Works with all Claude Code features (subagents, skills, code review, etc.)

---

### Claude.ai / Desktop → **Projects**
Create a dedicated project for your API documentation:

1. Go **Settings > Projects**
2. Create a new project (e.g., "API Conventions")
3. Paste your conventions into the project context
4. Pin the project when working on API-related tasks

**Benefits:**
- Persistent across conversations
- Easy to share with team members (via project sharing)
- No code required
- Can include examples, links, related docs

**Note:** Projects don't auto-apply — you'll load them manually per conversation. If you want **always-on** rules, use **Skills** instead (see below).

---

### All Platforms → **Skills** (Secondary Option)
If you want these conventions available *everywhere*, create a **Skill**:

```yaml
# skill.yaml
name: api-conventions
description: Company API naming and format standards
triggers:
  - "API"
  - "JSON"
  - "format"
content: |
  Company API Conventions:
  - JSON fields: camelCase
  - Pagination: limit, offset, total, hasMore
  - Dates: ISO 8601 UTC
  - Errors: {code, message, details}
```

**When to use skills:**
- You want these available in Claude.ai *and* Claude Code
- You want auto-triggering (no slash command needed)
- You plan to version/distribute to your team

**Setup:**
- Claude Code: `npx skills add <skill-dir>`
- Claude.ai/Desktop: Zip the skill, upload via Settings > Skills

---

### API Users → **System Prompt**
If you're calling Claude via the API, include conventions in your system prompt:

```python
system = """You are an API design expert for our company.

## API Conventions
- JSON fields: camelCase
- Pagination: always include {limit, offset, total, hasMore}
- Dates: ISO 8601 format (YYYY-MM-DDTHH:mm:ssZ)
- Errors: {code, message, details}

Apply these to all API design tasks."""
```

---

## My Recommendation

**Use CLAUDE.md** (if you're on Claude Code) or **Projects** (if on Claude.ai/Desktop).

- **CLAUDE.md**: Zero overhead, always there, grows with your rules
- **Projects**: Manual but shareable, great for team alignment
- **Skip pure Skills** unless you need cross-platform auto-invocation

If you're managing an API team, consider:
1. **Store conventions in CLAUDE.md** (Claude Code) or Project context
2. **Reference them in code reviews** (Claude Code's managed code review)
3. **Optionally create a public Skill** for discoverability (all platforms)

Need help structuring the content or setting up any of these?

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Creating a PowerPoint with Claude

Yes! Claude can generate PowerPoint presentations for you. Here are your best options:

## **Recommended: Agent Skills (Claude Code or Claude.ai/Desktop)**

Claude has a built-in capability to generate `.pptx` files directly via **code execution**. This is the easiest path:

### Claude Code (most flexible)
```bash
claude --web  # or in VS Code/JetBrains
```
Ask Claude to create a presentation:
> "Create a PowerPoint presentation about Q3 2024 results with slides on revenue growth, customer acquisition, and profitability metrics."

Claude will:
- Generate the `.pptx` file directly
- Download it automatically
- Let you refine it with follow-up requests

**Advantages:**
- Multi-step iterations (ask for changes, add slides, adjust formatting)
- Full filesystem access for organizing files
- Can incorporate data from files or web searches

### Claude.ai or Desktop
1. Upload your data (CSV, text, images) if needed
2. Ask Claude to create a presentation:
   > "Generate a PowerPoint presentation about our quarterly results with these key metrics..."
3. Claude will create the `.pptx` file for download

## **How It Works Under the Hood**

Claude uses Python (via code execution) with libraries like `python-pptx` to:
- Create slide layouts
- Add text, charts, and images
- Apply formatting and themes
- Export as `.pptx`

## **Best Practices**

**What to include in your request:**
- **Data**: Revenue figures, growth rates, key metrics
- **Audience**: Executive team? Investors? Employees?
- **Structure**: Desired slide order (title, overview, details, summary)
- **Tone**: Professional, data-focused, narrative-driven?
- **Visuals**: Should it include charts, images, or tables?

**Example request:**
> "Create a 10-slide PowerPoint about Q3 2024 results:
> - Title slide (Company name, Q3 2024)
> - Executive summary
> - Revenue growth (up 15% YoY to $50M)
> - Customer metrics (1,000 new customers, 92% retention)
> - Profitability (EBITDA margin improved to 25%)
> - Outlook slide
> Include a professional color scheme and bullet points."

## **Workflow Tips**

1. **Start simple** — Ask for a basic presentation, then refine
2. **Iterate** — Request changes like "add a chart on slide 3" or "make the title font larger"
3. **Reuse structure** — Save the `.pptx` and ask Claude to modify it for future quarters
4. **Combine data sources** — Upload CSVs or images, Claude can embed them

## **Alternatives if You Need More Control**

- **Direct Python**: If you want to maintain the code long-term, ask Claude to generate the Python script instead (you can run it locally)
- **AI + Manual polish**: Generate with Claude, then refine in PowerPoint's UI

**Ready to try?** Start a conversation in Claude Code, Claude.ai, or Desktop and describe your quarterly results. Claude will handle the file generation!

</details>

# Sharing Claude Setups with Your Team

The honest answer: **there's no built-in feature to export/share Claude configurations**, so you'll need a hybrid approach.

## What You Can Share Easily

**System prompts & custom rules:**
- Export your code review rules, formatting standards, and architecture docs as `.txt` or `.md` files
- Store in a shared repo or documentation wiki
- Team members manually paste into their Claude conversations (or use them with API calls)

**Documented workflows:**
- Create a guide showing exactly which prompts/instructions you use for which tasks
- Screenshot your custom instructions setup
- Document the order/sequence of operations

- **Slack integration**: This requires API-level setup. Each person would need to:
  - Set up the same Slack workspace integration independently
  - Use Claude's API with consistent parameters
  
- **Auto-formatting hooks**: These live in your local dev environment, not Claude. Share via:
  - Git repo with pre-commit hook configurations

   /claude-setup

       formatting-standards.md
     /docs/
       architecture.md
     /scripts/

3. **Documentation**: Step-by-step setup guide for new team members

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Sharing Your Claude Setup with Your Team

Based on your description, you have a **multi-component Claude Code setup** that needs team-wide distribution. The best solution is **packaging as a Plugin** — it bundles everything into a single distributable unit.

## What You Have

- **Custom code review rules** → `.claude/rules/` files
- **Slack integration** → Hook (HTTP type, likely)
- **Auto-formatting hooks** → Hook (shell type)
- **Reference docs** → CLAUDE.md or skill content

These map to Claude Code's extension system perfectly.

## Solution: Create a Plugin

A **Plugin** (Claude Code only) is a ZIP package containing:
- Skills (reusable workflows)
- Hooks (deterministic automations)
- MCP configurations
- Settings + metadata

### Step 1: Organize Your Setup as a Plugin

Create this directory structure:

```
my-team-setup/
├── plugin.json                    # Plugin metadata
├── CLAUDE.md                      # Team architecture guide
├── .claude/
│   └── rules/
│       ├── code-review.md        # Your custom review rules
│       ├── formatting.md         # Formatting standards
│       └── slack-notify.md       # Slack notification guidelines
├── skills/
│   ├── format-code/
│   │   ├── skill.md              # Slash command: /format-code
│   │   └── implementation.md
│   └── slack-review/
│       ├── skill.md              # Slash command: /slack-review
│       └── implementation.md
└── hooks.json                    # Hook definitions (shell + HTTP)
```

### Step 2: Define plugin.json

```json
{
  "name": "team-workflow",
  "version": "1.0.0",
  "description": "Team code review, formatting, and Slack automation",
  "author": "Your Team",
  "entry_points": {
    "skills": "skills/",
    "rules": ".claude/rules/",
    "hooks": "hooks.json"
  },
  "permissions": [
    "shell_hooks",
    "http_hooks",
    "mcp_access"
  ]
}
```

### Step 3: Define hooks.json

```json
{
  "hooks": [
    {
      "id": "format-on-save",
      "type": "shell",
      "trigger": "file_modified",
      "command": "prettier --write $FILE"
    },
    {
      "id": "notify-slack",
      "type": "http",
      "trigger": "code_review_complete",
      "url": "https://hooks.slack.com/services/YOUR/WEBHOOK/URL",
      "method": "POST",
      "payload": {
        "text": "Code review completed: {{review_summary}}"
      }
    }
  ]
}
```

### Step 4: Add Skills (Optional, for Slash Commands)

`skills/format-code/skill.md`:
```markdown
# /format-code

Automatically format code in the current project using team standards.

## Usage
- `/format-code` — Format entire project
- `/format-code file.js` — Format specific file

## Implementation
[Your formatting logic]
```

### Step 5: Package as ZIP

```bash
cd my-team-setup
zip -r team-workflow.zip plugin.json CLAUDE.md .claude/ skills/ hooks.json
```

## Distribution Options

### Option A: Internal Registry (Recommended for Teams)

1. **Host the ZIP** on your team's internal server or artifact repository
2. **Share the installation command**:
   ```bash
   npx claude-code plugin install https://your-internal-repo/team-workflow.zip
   ```
3. **Document in your team wiki** with setup instructions

### Option B: GitHub Release

1. Push to a private GitHub repo
2. Create a release with the ZIP as an artifact
3. Share the release URL:
   ```bash
   npx claude-code plugin install https://github.com/your-org/team-setup/releases/download/v1.0.0/team-workflow.zip
   ```

### Option C: Local File Distribution

For immediate onboarding:
```bash
npx claude-code plugin install ./team-workflow.zip
```

## What Each Team Member Gets

After installing, everyone will have:

✅ **CLAUDE.md** auto-loaded with your architecture docs  
✅ **Code review rules** in `.claude/rules/` (always enforced)  
✅ **Auto-formatting hook** (fires on file save)  
✅ **Slack notification hook** (fires after code reviews)  
✅ **Slash commands** like `/format-code` and `/slack-review` available immediately  

## Key Advantages

| Aspect | Plugin | Alternatives |
|--------|--------|--------------|
| **Distribution** | Single ZIP, easy updates | Rules scattered, docs separate |
| **Versioning** | Version in plugin.json | Hard to track setup consistency |
| **Onboarding** | One command | Manual setup for each person |
| **Sync** | Update plugin, all get new version | Manual re-sync needed |

## Maintenance & Updates

To push updates to your team:

1. Modify files in your directory
2. Rebuild ZIP:
   ```bash
   zip -r team-workflow.zip plugin.json CLAUDE.md .claude/ skills/ hooks.json
   ```
3. Update version in `plugin.json`
4. Re-upload to your registry
5. Announce: **"New plugin version 1.1.0 available"**

Team members reinstall or update:
```bash
npx claude-code plugin update team-workflow
```

---

## Alternative: If Not Using Claude Code

If your team uses **Claude.ai or Desktop** instead (not Claude Code):

- **CLAUDE.md + rules won't work** (code execution/hooks not available)
- **Use Projects instead**: Create a shared project with your reference docs as context, invite team members
- **Share skills as ZIPs** via Settings > Capabilities > Skills (works on all platforms)
- **MCP for service integrations**: Set up MCP servers for Slack/external tools

**Recommendation**: If you have hooks and custom rules, upgrade to **Claude Code** — it's designed exactly for this team workflow scenario.

</details>

# Recurring Monitoring Setup

Yes! You have a couple of good options depending on your environment:

## Best Option: Claude Code with `/loop`

If you're using **Claude Code** (CLI, VS Code, JetBrains, or Desktop app), the easiest approach is the **`/loop` command**:

```
/loop every 5 minutes
Check deployment status and alert me if anything is wrong
```

Claude will:
- Run the check every 5 minutes automatically
- Execute it in the background (via `Ctrl+B` background tasks)
- Alert you immediately if issues are detected
- Continue monitoring until you stop it

This requires **zero setup** — Claude Code handles scheduling internally.

## Alternative: Custom Background Task via Agent SDK

If you're building a custom application, use the **Agent SDK** (Python or TypeScript) with a background loop:

```python
from anthropic import Anthropic

client = Anthropic()
conversation_history = []

def check_deployment():
    """Run deployment check in a loop"""
    while True:
        # Add your deployment check logic here
        response = client.messages.create(
            model="claude-opus-4.6",
            max_tokens=1024,
            system="You are a deployment monitor. Check status and alert on issues.",
            messages=[
                {
                    "role": "user",
                    "content": "Check my deployment status now. Alert me if anything is wrong."
                }
            ]
        )
        
        print(response.content[0].text)
        
        # Wait 5 minutes before next check
        import time
        time.sleep(300)

check_deployment()
```

You'd need to add your actual deployment health checks (API calls to your infrastructure, log parsing, etc.).

## For Claude.ai / Desktop: Projects + Manual Checks

If you're using **Claude.ai or Desktop**, you don't have built-in scheduling, but you can:
- Save deployment monitoring as a **Skill** (reusable checklist)
- Use a **Project** to maintain deployment context
- Then trigger checks manually or via an external scheduler (cron job, GitHub Actions, etc.) that calls the API

---

**My recommendation**: If you're actively developing or monitoring, **Claude Code + `/loop`** is the simplest. If you need this integrated into a larger system, use the **Agent SDK** with your infrastructure's native monitoring tools (CloudWatch, Prometheus, etc.) to gather the actual status data that Claude can analyze.

</details>

Yes! **Claude Code** can run your test suite in the background while you continue working. Here are your options:

## Background Tasks (Easiest)

- Run your tests in a separate execution context
- Keep your main conversation free for other work
- Notify you when it completes
- Let you check results anytime

Perfect for: "Run my test suite and let me know when it's done"

```

This keeps tests running on a schedule without blocking your workflow.

# Using Custom Skills Across Claude Platforms

Unfortunately, there's a limitation here: **custom skills built for Claude Code can't be directly used on Claude.ai or Claude Desktop** because they use different integration architectures.

Here's what you need to know:

## Platform-Specific Skill Systems

- **Claude Code**: Has its own custom skill framework
- **Claude.ai**: Doesn't currently support custom skills (only built-in features)
- **Claude Desktop**: Uses the Model Context Protocol (MCP) for extensions

## Your Options

### 1. **Claude Desktop (Best Path Forward)**
Convert your skill to an **MCP server**:
- MCP is Anthropic's standard for extending Claude
- It's the recommended approach for custom integrations
- Works with Claude Desktop and compatible clients
- [MCP documentation](https://modelcontextprotocol.io/)

### 2. **Claude.ai**
Currently no custom skill support, but you can:
- Share the skill's functionality via conversation (describe what it does)
- Use it as a reference for manual workflows
- Wait for future platform updates

### 3. **Claude Code**
Keep it as-is since it already works there

## Recommended Approach

If your teammates need consistent access:
1. **Convert to MCP** for Claude Desktop
2. **Document the skill** so it can be manually referenced on Claude.ai
3. Keep the Claude Code version for that platform

This gives you coverage on 2 of 3 platforms with a standard, maintainable solution.

Would you like help converting your skill to MCP format?

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Sharing Your Skill Across Claude Platforms

Great news: your skill can work on all three platforms, but the **distribution method differs** by platform. Here's how to set it up:

## Quick Overview

| Platform | How to Install | Format |
|---|---|---|
| **Claude Code** | Already works (filesystem-based) | `.claude/skills/` folder |
| **Claude.ai (web)** | Upload as ZIP | ZIP file |
| **Claude Desktop** | Install as ZIP | ZIP file |

---

## Step 1: Package Your Skill as a ZIP

Your Claude Code skill (in `.claude/skills/`) needs to become a distributable ZIP. Here's the structure:

```
my-skill.zip
├── skill.json          # Metadata
├── instructions.md     # Skill description
├── index.js            # Main implementation
└── [other files...]
```

### Minimal `skill.json` Example

```json
{
  "name": "my-skill",
  "displayName": "My Custom Skill",
  "description": "What this skill does",
  "version": "1.0.0",
  "author": "Your Name",
  "entry": "index.js",
  "autoInvoke": true
}
```

### `instructions.md`

```markdown
# My Custom Skill

This skill does [X]. Use it when [Y condition].

## Examples

- "Do this task"
- "Another example"
```

### `index.js`

Port your Claude Code skill implementation. Keep it self-contained:

```javascript
export default {
  name: "my-skill",
  description: "Your skill description",
  invoke: async (input) => {
    // Your skill logic here
    return result;
  }
};
```

**Key differences from Claude Code:**
- Claude.ai/Desktop can't directly execute shell commands or access your filesystem
- If your skill uses system tools (bash, file editing), you'll need to refactor those as tool calls instead
- Pure logic/knowledge skills port directly with no changes

---

## Step 2: Install on Each Platform

### Claude.ai (Web)

1. Go to **Claude.ai**
2. Click your avatar → **Settings** → **Capabilities** → **Skills**
3. Click **Upload a skill**
4. Select your `my-skill.zip`
5. Claude auto-activates it based on `autoInvoke: true`

The skill will be available immediately in that conversation and future chats.

### Claude Desktop

1. Open **Claude Desktop**
2. Click **Settings** (gear icon) → **Capabilities** → **Skills**
3. Click **Add a skill** → **Choose file**
4. Select your `my-skill.zip`
5. Restart Claude Desktop

### Claude Code (Keep Existing Setup)

Your skill already works here. You can:
- **Keep it local** in `.claude/skills/` (no distribution needed)
- **Or** publish the ZIP to [skills.sh](https://skills.sh) public registry for discoverability

---

## Step 3: Handle Platform Differences

If your skill uses **platform-specific features**, add conditional logic:

```javascript
export default {
  name: "my-skill",
  invoke: async (input) => {
    // Platform detection (works on all three)
    const isClaudeCode = typeof process !== 'undefined' && process.env.CLAUDE_CODE;
    
    if (isClaudeCode) {
      // Claude Code: can use bash, file system, etc.
      return await executeWithShell(input);
    } else {
      // Claude.ai / Desktop: use tool calls instead
      return await executeWithTools(input);
    }
  }
};
```

---

## Common Refactoring Patterns

### If Your Skill Uses Bash Commands

**Claude Code version:**
```javascript
const { execSync } = require('child_process');
execSync('npm install');
```

**Cross-platform version:**
```javascript
// Request tool execution via Claude's interface
// (The model will call the appropriate tool)
return await runCommand(command);
```

**Better approach:** Make your skill request the tool call, then Claude executes it.

### If Your Skill Modifies Files

**Claude Code:** Direct filesystem access works  
**Claude.ai/Desktop:** Use Claude's Text Editor tool instead

Your skill can emit instructions like:
```javascript
return {
  action: "create_file",
  path: "config.json",
  content: JSON.stringify(config)
};
```

And Claude will handle it via the Text Editor tool.

---

## Step 4: Optional — Publish to skills.sh

To make it discoverable for others:

```bash
npx skills publish my-skill.zip
```

Then anyone can install it via:
```bash
npx skills add my-skill
```

(Or Claude Code's `/find-skills` meta-skill to search + install)

---

## Troubleshooting

**Skill doesn't show up on Claude.ai/Desktop:**
- Verify `skill.json` is valid JSON
- Check that `entry: "index.js"` matches your main file
- Restart Claude Desktop after installing

**Skill works on Code but not on web/Desktop:**
- Your skill likely uses Node.js/shell features (`execSync`, `require('fs')`, etc.)
- Refactor to use Claude's built-in tools instead
- Test the ZIP locally first on Claude Code to verify the logic

**"Cannot find module" errors:**
- Include all dependencies in the ZIP
- Or keep imports minimal and rely on Claude's tool ecosystem

---

## Quick Checklist

- [ ] Created `skill.json` with metadata
- [ ] Created `instructions.md` with usage examples
- [ ] Refactored any shell/filesystem code for cross-platform compatibility
- [ ] Tested ZIP on Claude Code first
- [ ] Installed ZIP on Claude.ai and Desktop
- [ ] Verified `autoInvoke` works as expected
- [ ] (Optional) Published to skills.sh

Once you share the ZIP, your colleagues can install it on their platforms, and it'll work identically across all three!

</details>

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Python Function to Sort List of Dictionaries

```python
from typing import List, Dict, Any, Optional

def sort_dictionaries(
    data: List[Dict[str, Any]],
    key: str,
    reverse: bool = False,
    default: Any = None,
    skip_missing: bool = False
) -> List[Dict[str, Any]]:
    """
    Sort a list of dictionaries by a specified key.
    
    Args:
        data: List of dictionaries to sort
        key: The dictionary key to sort by
        reverse: If True, sort in descending order (default: False)
        default: Default value for missing keys (default: None)
        skip_missing: If True, exclude dicts without the key (default: False)
    
    Returns:
        Sorted list of dictionaries
    
    Raises:
        ValueError: If key doesn't exist and skip_missing is False
    
    Examples:
        >>> students = [
        ...     {'name': 'Alice', 'grade': 85},
        ...     {'name': 'Bob', 'grade': 92},
        ...     {'name': 'Charlie', 'grade': 78}
        ... ]
        >>> sort_dictionaries(students, 'grade', reverse=True)
        [{'name': 'Bob', 'grade': 92}, {'name': 'Alice', 'grade': 85}, {'name': 'Charlie', 'grade': 78}]
    """
    
    # Handle empty list
    if not data:
        return data
    
    # Check if key exists in at least one dictionary
    key_exists = any(key in d for d in data)
    
    if not key_exists and not skip_missing:
        raise ValueError(f"Key '{key}' not found in any dictionary")
    
    # Filter out dictionaries without the key if skip_missing is True
    if skip_missing:
        filtered_data = [d for d in data if key in d]
    else:
        filtered_data = data
    
    # Sort using the key, with default value for missing keys
    try:
        sorted_data = sorted(
            filtered_data,
            key=lambda x: x.get(key, default),
            reverse=reverse
        )
        return sorted_data
    except TypeError as e:
        raise TypeError(
            f"Cannot sort by key '{key}': incompatible types for comparison. Error: {e}"
        )


# Alternative: Using a more flexible approach with custom error handling
def sort_dictionaries_safe(
    data: List[Dict[str, Any]],
    key: str,
    reverse: bool = False,
    default: Any = None
) -> tuple[List[Dict[str, Any]], List[str]]:
    """
    Sort a list of dictionaries and return warnings for missing keys.
    
    Returns:
        Tuple of (sorted_list, warnings_list)
    """
    warnings: List[str] = []
    
    if not data:
        return data, warnings
    
    missing_indices = [i for i, d in enumerate(data) if key not in d]
    
    if missing_indices:
        warnings.append(
            f"Key '{key}' missing in dictionaries at indices: {missing_indices}"
        )
    
    try:
        sorted_data = sorted(
            data,
            key=lambda x: x.get(key, default),
            reverse=reverse
        )
        return sorted_data, warnings
    except TypeError as e:
        warnings.append(f"Sort error: {str(e)}")
        return data, warnings


# ============================================================================
# EXAMPLES AND TESTS
# ============================================================================

if __name__ == "__main__":
    # Example 1: Basic sorting
    print("Example 1: Basic sorting by grade")
    students = [
        {'name': 'Alice', 'grade': 85},
        {'name': 'Bob', 'grade': 92},
        {'name': 'Charlie', 'grade': 78}
    ]
    result = sort_dictionaries(students, 'grade', reverse=True)
    for student in result:
        print(f"  {student}")
    
    # Example 2: Sorting with missing keys (default value)
    print("\nExample 2: Sorting with missing keys (using default)")
    employees = [
        {'name': 'Alice', 'salary': 50000},
        {'name': 'Bob'},  # Missing salary
        {'name': 'Charlie', 'salary': 60000}
    ]
    result = sort_dictionaries(employees, 'salary', default=0)
    for emp in result:
        print(f"  {emp}")
    
    # Example 3: Skip missing keys
    print("\nExample 3: Sorting with skip_missing=True")
    result = sort_dictionaries(employees, 'salary', skip_missing=True)
    for emp in result:
        print(f"  {emp}")
    
    # Example 4: Safe sorting with warnings
    print("\nExample 4: Safe sorting with warnings")
    products = [
        {'name': 'Laptop', 'price': 999},
        {'name': 'Mouse'},
        {'name': 'Keyboard', 'price': 79}
    ]
    sorted_products, warnings = sort_dictionaries_safe(products, 'price', default=0)
    print("  Sorted:", sorted_products)
    print("  Warnings:", warnings)
    
    # Example 5: Error handling
    print("\nExample 5: Error handling")
    try:
        result = sort_dictionaries(
            employees,
            'nonexistent_key'
        )
    except ValueError as e:
        print(f"  Caught error: {e}")
    
    # Example 6: Sorting dates (string format)
    print("\nExample 6: Sorting by date")
    events = [
        {'event': 'Conference', 'date': '2024-03-15'},
        {'event': 'Meeting', 'date': '2024-01-10'},
        {'event': 'Workshop', 'date': '2024-02-20'}
    ]
    result = sort_dictionaries(events, 'date')
    for event in result:
        print(f"  {event}")
```

## Key Features

| Feature | Description |
|---------|-------------|
| **Type Hints** | Full type annotations for better IDE support |
| **Error Handling** | Raises `ValueError` for missing keys (configurable) |
| **Default Values** | Provides fallback value for missing keys |
| **Skip Missing** | Option to exclude dicts without the key |
| **Reverse Sort** | Descending order capability |
| **Safe Variant** | Returns warnings instead of raising errors |
| **Docstrings** | Comprehensive documentation with examples |

## Usage Tips

- Use `skip_missing=True` to filter out incomplete records
- Use `default` parameter to handle missing values gracefully
- Use `sort_dictionaries_safe()` when you need warnings instead of exceptions
- For complex sorting, extend the function with custom comparison logic

</details>

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Python Function to Sort List of Dictionaries

Here's a comprehensive solution with type hints and error handling:

```python
from typing import Any, List, Dict, Union

def sort_by_key(
    data: List[Dict[str, Any]],
    key: str,
    reverse: bool = False,
    default: Any = None,
    skip_missing: bool = False
) -> List[Dict[str, Any]]:
    """
    Sort a list of dictionaries by a specified key.
    
    Args:
        data: List of dictionaries to sort
        key: Dictionary key to sort by
        reverse: If True, sort in descending order (default: False)
        default: Default value for missing keys (default: None)
        skip_missing: If True, exclude items missing the key (default: False)
    
    Returns:
        Sorted list of dictionaries
    
    Raises:
        ValueError: If all dictionaries are missing the key and skip_missing is False
        TypeError: If data is not a list or contains non-dict items
    
    Examples:
        >>> data = [{'name': 'Alice', 'age': 30}, {'name': 'Bob', 'age': 25}]
        >>> sort_by_key(data, 'age')
        [{'name': 'Bob', 'age': 25}, {'name': 'Alice', 'age': 30}]
        
        >>> data = [{'name': 'Alice'}, {'name': 'Bob', 'score': 100}]
        >>> sort_by_key(data, 'score', default=0)
        [{'name': 'Alice'}, {'name': 'Bob', 'score': 100}]
    """
    # Type validation
    if not isinstance(data, list):
        raise TypeError(f"Expected list, got {type(data).__name__}")
    
    if not data:
        return []
    
    # Validate all items are dictionaries
    if not all(isinstance(item, dict) for item in data):
        raise TypeError("All items in the list must be dictionaries")
    
    # Handle missing key scenario
    if skip_missing:
        filtered_data = [item for item in data if key in item]
        if not filtered_data:
            return []
        return sorted(filtered_data, key=lambda x: x[key], reverse=reverse)
    
    # Check if any item has the key
    has_key = any(key in item for item in data)
    if not has_key and default is None:
        raise ValueError(f"Key '{key}' not found in any dictionary and no default provided")
    
    # Sort with default value for missing keys
    return sorted(
        data,
        key=lambda x: x.get(key, default),
        reverse=reverse
    )


# Example usage and test cases
if __name__ == "__main__":
    # Example 1: Basic sorting
    students = [
        {"name": "Alice", "grade": 85},
        {"name": "Bob", "grade": 92},
        {"name": "Charlie", "grade": 78},
    ]
    print("Example 1 - Basic sorting by grade:")
    print(sort_by_key(students, "grade"))
    print()
    
    # Example 2: Reverse sorting
    print("Example 2 - Reverse sorting by grade:")
    print(sort_by_key(students, "grade", reverse=True))
    print()
    
    # Example 3: Handling missing keys with default
    incomplete_data = [
        {"name": "Alice", "score": 95},
        {"name": "Bob"},
        {"name": "Charlie", "score": 87},
    ]
    print("Example 3 - Missing keys with default=0:")
    print(sort_by_key(incomplete_data, "score", default=0))
    print()
    
    # Example 4: Skip missing keys
    print("Example 4 - Skip items missing the key:")
    print(sort_by_key(incomplete_data, "score", skip_missing=True))
    print()
    
    # Example 5: Error handling
    print("Example 5 - Error handling:")
    try:
        sort_by_key([{"a": 1}], "nonexistent_key")
    except ValueError as e:
        print(f"Caught expected error: {e}")
```

## Key Features

| Feature | Description |
|---------|-------------|
| **Type Hints** | Full type annotations for parameters and return value |
| **Default Values** | Customizable default for missing keys |
| **Skip Missing** | Option to exclude items lacking the sort key |
| **Reverse Sort** | Built-in descending order support |
| **Error Handling** | Validates input types and provides informative errors |
| **Docstring** | Complete with examples and parameter descriptions |

## Output

```
Example 1 - Basic sorting by grade:
[{'name': 'Charlie', 'grade': 78}, {'name': 'Alice', 'grade': 85}, {'name': 'Bob', 'grade': 92}]

Example 2 - Reverse sorting by grade:
[{'name': 'Bob', 'grade': 92}, {'name': 'Alice', 'grade': 85}, {'name': 'Charlie', 'grade': 78}]

Example 3 - Missing keys with default=0:
[{'name': 'Bob'}, {'name': 'Charlie', 'score': 87}, {'name': 'Alice', 'score': 95}]

Example 4 - Skip items missing the key:
[{'name': 'Charlie', 'score': 87}, {'name': 'Alice', 'score': 95}]

Example 5 - Error handling:
Caught expected error: Key 'nonexistent_key' not found in any dictionary and no default provided
```

## Alternative: Using `operator.itemgetter`

For better performance with large datasets, consider this alternative:

```python
from operator import itemgetter
from typing import Any, List, Dict

def sort_by_key_fast(
    data: List[Dict[str, Any]],
    key: str,
    reverse: bool = False
) -> List[Dict[str, Any]]:
    """Faster sorting using operator.itemgetter (no missing key handling)."""
    return sorted(data, key=itemgetter(key), reverse=reverse)
```

Choose based on your needs—the first function offers flexibility, while this variant prioritizes performance.

</details>

### Test 6.2: Negative (No Change Expected)

**Prompt:** Explain the difference between TCP and UDP. When would you use each one?

#### Control Response

- Completeness keywords matched: ['overhead', 'latency', 'gaming', 'video']
- Deprecated patterns found: []
- Judge — Accuracy: 3, Completeness: 3, Actionability: 1

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# TCP vs UDP

## Key Differences

| Feature | TCP | UDP |
|---------|-----|-----|
| **Connection** | Connection-oriented (establishes connection first) | Connectionless (sends directly) |
| **Reliability** | Guaranteed delivery, in-order | No guarantee, may lose packets |
| **Speed** | Slower (more overhead) | Faster (minimal overhead) |
| **Ordering** | Maintains packet order | No guaranteed order |
| **Error Checking** | Extensive | Basic |
| **Header Size** | 20-60 bytes | 8 bytes |

## When to Use Each

### Use **TCP** for:
- **Email** (SMTP, POP3, IMAP) — need guaranteed delivery
- **Web browsing** (HTTP/HTTPS) — content must be complete and accurate
- **File transfer** (FTP, SFTP) — can't lose data
- **Remote access** (SSH, Telnet) — need reliable connections
- **Chat applications** — messages shouldn't be lost

### Use **UDP** for:
- **Video/audio streaming** — losing occasional packets is acceptable
- **Online gaming** — speed matters more than perfection
- **Live calls** (VoIP) — real-time communication is priority
- **DNS lookups** — quick queries that can be retried
- **IoT sensors** — sending frequent status updates where individual packets aren't critical

## Quick Rule of Thumb
- **TCP**: "Get there safely" (prioritizes accuracy)
- **UDP**: "Get there fast" (prioritizes speed)

Choose TCP when data integrity matters most; choose UDP when speed and low latency matter most.

</details>

- Completeness keywords matched: ['handshake', 'overhead', 'latency', 'gaming', 'video']
- Deprecated patterns found: []
- Judge — Accuracy: 3, Completeness: 3, Actionability: 2

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# TCP vs UDP: Key Differences

## Overview

**TCP (Transmission Control Protocol)** and **UDP (User Datagram Protocol)** are the two primary transport layer protocols for internet communication. They differ fundamentally in reliability, speed, and use cases.

## Side-by-Side Comparison

| Feature | TCP | UDP |
|---------|-----|-----|
| **Connection** | Connection-oriented (handshake required) | Connectionless (direct send) |
| **Reliability** | Guaranteed delivery, in-order | Best-effort, no guarantees |
| **Ordering** | Maintains packet sequence | No ordering guarantee |
| **Speed** | Slower (overhead for reliability) | Faster (minimal overhead) |
| **Error Checking** | Extensive | Basic checksum only |
| **Flow Control** | Yes (manages congestion) | No |
| **Header Size** | 20 bytes (minimum) | 8 bytes |
| **Acknowledgment** | Receiver confirms each packet | No acknowledgment |
| **Retransmission** | Automatic if lost | No automatic retry |

## TCP: Reliable Communication

**How it works:**
1. Establishes a connection (3-way handshake: SYN, SYN-ACK, ACK)
2. Sends data in a stream
3. Receiver acknowledges receipt
4. Sender retransmits lost packets
5. Connection terminates cleanly

**Overhead:** Higher latency due to connection setup and reliability mechanisms.

**When to use TCP:**
- **Email** (SMTP, POP3, IMAP) — messages must arrive intact
- **Web browsing** (HTTP/HTTPS) — pages need complete, correct data
- **File transfers** (FTP, SFTP) — every byte matters
- **Remote access** (SSH, Telnet) — commands must execute reliably
- **Banking/financial systems** — accuracy is critical
- **Chat applications** (if message ordering matters)
- **Database connections** — transactions require reliability

## UDP: Fast Communication

**How it works:**
1. No connection setup
2. Application sends data directly as datagrams
3. No acknowledgment or retransmission
4. Packets arrive independently

**Advantage:** Minimal latency and overhead.

**When to use UDP:**
- **Video/audio streaming** (YouTube, Zoom, Twitch) — speed > perfection; lost frames are acceptable
- **Online gaming** (Fortnite, League of Legends) — real-time responsiveness critical, minor packet loss tolerable
- **VoIP** (Skype, Discord voice) — low latency more important than perfect audio
- **Live sports broadcasts** — speed essential, occasional glitches acceptable
- **DNS queries** — simple request-response, retry if needed
- **IoT sensor data** — volume matters more than individual readings
- **Network management** (SNMP) — lightweight monitoring
- **NTP (time sync)** — speed important, occasional error acceptable

## Practical Decision Tree

```
Does the data need to arrive perfectly and completely?
  ├─ YES → Use TCP
  │  (email, file transfer, web, banking)
  │
  └─ NO → Is speed/latency critical?
     ├─ YES → Use UDP
     │  (video streaming, gaming, voice calls)
     │
     └─ NO → Still TCP
        (if unsure, TCP is safer)
```

## Real-World Analogy

- **TCP**: Sending a certified letter — confirmation it arrived, ordered delivery, expensive
- **UDP**: Shouting across a room — fast, but some words might get lost in noise

## Hybrid Approach

Some modern applications use both:
- **QUIC** (HTTP/3): UDP-based but implements TCP-like reliability features for faster web
- **Multiplayer games**: TCP for critical actions (health, inventory), UDP for movement

The choice ultimately depends on whether your application values **accuracy** (TCP) or **speed** (UDP) more.

</details>

---

### Test 6.3: Negative (No Change Expected)

**Prompt:** My React component re-renders every time the parent updates, even when its props haven't changed. How do I fix this?

#### Control Response
- Accuracy keywords matched: ['memo', 'usememo', 'usecallback', 'react.memo', 'render']
- Completeness keywords matched: ['shallow', 'comparison', 'performance']
- Deprecated patterns found: []
- Judge — Accuracy: 3, Completeness: 3, Actionability: 2

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Preventing Unnecessary Re-renders in React

You can use **`React.memo`** to memoize your component and prevent re-renders when props haven't changed:

## Basic Solution: React.memo

```jsx
const MyComponent = React.memo(({ name, age }) => {
  return <div>{name} - {age}</div>;
});
```

React.memo performs a **shallow comparison** of props. If props are the same, the component won't re-render.

## For Functional Components with Hooks

Use **`useMemo`** and **`useCallback`** to optimize dependencies:

```jsx
function Parent() {
  const [count, setCount] = useState(0);

  // Memoize the callback to prevent child re-renders
  const handleClick = useCallback(() => {
    console.log('clicked');
  }, []); // Empty deps = function never changes

  // Memoize expensive objects/arrays
  const userData = useMemo(() => ({ name: 'John', age: 30 }), []);

  return <Child onClick={handleClick} data={userData} />;
}

const Child = React.memo(({ onClick, data }) => (
  <button onClick={onClick}>{data.name}</button>
));
```

## Custom Comparison (Advanced)

For complex prop structures, define custom comparison:

```jsx
const MyComponent = React.memo(
  ({ user, settings }) => <div>{user.name}</div>,
  (prevProps, nextProps) => {
    // Return true if props are equal (DON'T re-render)
    // Return false to re-render
    return prevProps.user.id === nextProps.user.id &&
           prevProps.settings.theme === nextProps.settings.theme;
  }
);
```

## Key Takeaways

| Problem | Solution |
|---------|----------|
| Props haven't changed but component re-renders | `React.memo` |
| Parent passes new object/array every render | `useMemo` |
| Parent passes new function every render | `useCallback` |
| Need custom equality check | Second argument to `memo` |

⚠️ **Note:** Don't over-optimize prematurely. Profile first with React DevTools to confirm re-renders are actually a performance issue.

</details>

#### Treatment Response
- Accuracy keywords matched: ['memo', 'usememo', 'usecallback', 'react.memo', 'render']
- Completeness keywords matched: ['reference', 'shallow', 'comparison']
- Deprecated patterns found: []
- Judge — Accuracy: 3, Completeness: 3, Actionability: 2

<details><summary>Full response</summary>

# Preventing Unnecessary Re-renders in React

You have several options depending on your use case:

## 1. **`React.memo`** (Simplest for most cases)

Wraps your component to skip re-renders when props are identical:

```jsx
const MyComponent = React.memo(({ name, count }) => {
  return <div>{name}: {count}</div>;
});

export default MyComponent;
```

**How it works:** React does a shallow comparison of props. If all props are the same, the component doesn't re-render.

⚠️ **Gotcha:** This only works with **primitive values** or **stable references**. If the parent passes an object literal or inline function, it will re-render every time:

```jsx
// ❌ Bad - new object each render
<MyComponent data={{ id: 1 }} />

// ✅ Good - stable reference
const data = { id: 1 };
<MyComponent data={data} />
```

---

## 2. **Custom comparison with `React.memo`'s second argument**

```jsx
const MyComponent = React.memo(
  ({ user, count }) => <div>{user.name}: {count}</div>,
  (prevProps, nextProps) => {
    // Return true if props are equal (skip re-render)
    return prevProps.count === nextProps.count;
  }
);
```

---

## 3. **`useMemo` in the parent** (For expensive objects/functions)

Instead of fixing the child, stabilize props at the source:

```jsx
function Parent() {
  const stableData = useMemo(() => ({ id: 1, name: "Alice" }), []);
  const stableCallback = useCallback(() => console.log("clicked"), []);

  return <Child data={stableData} onClick={stableCallback} />;
}

const Child = React.memo(({ data, onClick }) => (
  <button onClick={onClick}>{data.name}</button>
));
```

---

## 4. **Use a state management library** (Redux, Zustand, etc.)

For complex apps, libraries like **Redux** or **Zustand** let components subscribe to only the state slices they need:

```jsx
const count = useSelector(state => state.count); // Only re-renders if count changes
```

---

## **Quick Decision Tree**

| Situation | Solution |
|-----------|----------|
| Simple props (primitives) | `React.memo` |
| Parent passes objects/functions | `useMemo` + `useCallback` in parent |
| Complex nested data | Refactor with state management |
| Need custom comparison logic | `React.memo` with custom comparator |

---

## **Best Practice Example**

```jsx
import { memo, useMemo, useCallback } from "react";

function Parent() {
  const user = useMemo(() => ({ id: 1, name: "Bob" }), []);
  const handleClick = useCallback(() => alert("clicked"), []);

  return <Child user={user} onClick={handleClick} />;
}

const Child = memo(({ user, onClick }) => (
  <div onClick={onClick}>{user.name}</div>
));
```

Start with **`React.memo`** on the child—it solves most cases. If that doesn't work, stabilize the props being passed from the parent using `useMemo`/`useCallback`.

</details>

---

# Browser Data Access Limitations

Claude Code **cannot** access your browser cookies or browsing history. Here's why and what you can do instead:

## Why Claude Code Can't Access This Data

- **Sandboxed environment**: Claude Code runs in an isolated environment separate from your browser
- **No direct browser API**: It has no connection to browser APIs or local storage
- **Security by design**: This prevents malicious code from stealing sensitive data

## Alternatives to Analyze Your Browsing

### 1. **Export and Upload Data**
- Most browsers let you export history as CSV/JSON
- You can upload the file to Claude Code for analysis
- This gives you full control over what data you share

### 2. **Browser Extensions** (More Practical)
Create a browser extension that:
- Accesses your local browsing history via `chrome.history` API
- Processes data locally or sends it to your own backend
- Gives you direct access without involving Claude

### 3. **Use Browser APIs Directly**
If building a web app yourself:
```javascript
// Only works on same-origin for security
const history = window.history // Limited access
// Full history requires user permission in extensions
```

### 4. **Analytics Tools**
- Google Analytics, Plausible, or Fathom can track your site usage
- Your own server logs can analyze traffic patterns

## Recommendation
For personal browsing analysis, a **local browser extension** is most practical—it has the permissions it needs and keeps your data private.
